<p>Cephalopods are a diverse group of species, but share common life history traits, for example they have a rapid growth rate and short life spans.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> Stearns (1992) suggested that in order to produce the largest possible number of viable offspring, spawning events depend on ecological environmental factors of the organism. The majority of cephalopods do not provide parental care to their offspring, except for example, octopus, which helps this organism increase the survival rate of their offspring.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_79-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> Marine species' life cycles are affected by various environmental conditions.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> The development of a cephalopod embryo can be greatly affected by temperature, oxygen saturation, pollution, light intensity, and salinity.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_79-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> These factors are important to the rate of embryonic development and the success of hatching of the embryos. Food availability also plays an important role in the reproductive cycle of cephalopods. A limitation of food influences the timing of spawning along with their function and growth.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_80-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> Spawning time and spawning vary among marine species; it's correlated with temperature, though cephalopods in shallow water spawn in cold months so that the offspring would hatch at warmer temperatures. Breeding can last from several days to a month.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_79-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Sexual_maturity">Sexual maturity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cephalopod&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Sexual maturity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Cephalopods that are sexually mature and of adult size begin spawning and reproducing. After the transfer of genetic material to the following generation, the adult cephalopods then die.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_79-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> Sexual maturation in male and female cephalopods can be observed internally by the enlargement of gonads and accessory glands.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceC_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceC-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> Mating would be a poor indicator of sexual maturation in females; they can receive sperm when not fully reproductively mature and store them until they are ready to fertilize the eggs.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_80-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> Males are more aggressive in their pre-mating competition when in the presence of immature females than when competing for a sexually mature female.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> Most cephalopod males develop a hectocotylus, an arm tip which is capable of transferring their spermatozoa into the female mantel cavity. Though not all species use a hectocotylus; for example, the adult nautilus releases a spadix.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> An indication of sexual maturity of females is the development of brachial photophores to attract mates.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Fertilization">Fertilization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cephalopod&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Fertilization">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Cephalopods are not broadcast spawners. During the process of fertilization, the females use sperm provided by the male via external fertilization. Internal fertilization is seen only in octopods.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceC_81-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceC-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> The initiation of copulation begins when the male catches a female and wraps his arm around her, either in a "male to female neck" position or mouth to mouth position, depending on the species. The males then initiate the process of fertilization by contracting their mantle several times to release the spermatozoa.<sup id="cite_ref-SquiresEtAl2013_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SquiresEtAl2013-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> Cephalopods often mate several times, which influences males to mate longer with females that have previously, nearly tripling the amount of contractions of the mantle.<sup id="cite_ref-SquiresEtAl2013_85-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SquiresEtAl2013-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> To ensure the fertilization of the eggs, female cephalopods release a sperm-attracting peptide through the gelatinous layers of the egg to direct the spermatozoa. Female cephalopods lay eggs in clutches; each egg is composed of a protective coat to ensure the safety of the developing embryo when released into the water column. Reproductive strategies differ between cephalopod species. In giant pacific octopus, large eggs are laid in a den; it will often take several days to lay all of them.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceC_81-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceC-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> Once the eggs are released and attached to a sheltered substrate, the females then die.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceC_81-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceC-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> In some species of cephalopods, egg clutches are anchored to substrates by a mucilaginous adhesive substance. These eggs are swelled with preivitelline fluid (PVF), a hypertonic fluid that prevents premature hatching.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> Fertilized egg clusters are neutrally buoyant depending on the depth that they were laid, but can also be found in substrates such as sand, matrix of corals, or seaweed.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_80-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> Because these species do not provide parental care for their offspring, egg capsules can be injected with ink by the female in order to camouflage the embryos from predators.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_80-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Male-male_competition">Male-male competition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cephalopod&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Male-male competition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Most cephalopods engage in aggressive sex: a protein in the male capsule sheath stimulates this behavior. They also engage in male-male aggression, where larger males tend to win the interactions.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_79-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> When a female is near, the males charge one another continuously and flail their arms. If neither male backs away, the arms extend to the back, exposing the mouth, followed by the biting of arm tips.<sup id="cite_ref-NormanEtAl1997_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NormanEtAl1997-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> During mate competition males also participate in a technique called flushing. This technique is used by the second male attempting to mate with a female. Flushing removes spermatophores in the buccal cavity that was placed there by the first mate by forcing water into the cavity.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_79-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> Another behavior that males engage in is sneaker mating or mimicry - smaller males adjust their behavior to that of a female in order to reduce aggression. By using this technique, they are able to fertilize the eggs while the larger male is distracted by different male.<sup id="cite_ref-NormanEtAl1997_87-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NormanEtAl1997-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> During this process, the sneaker males quickly insert drop-like sperm into the seminal receptacle.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Mate_choice">Mate choice</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cephalopod&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Mate choice">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Mate choice is seen in cuttlefish species, where females prefer some males over others, though characteristics of the preferred males are unknown.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_79-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> A hypothesis states that females reject males by olfactory cues rather than visual cues.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_79-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> Several cephalopod species are polyandrous- accepting and storing multiple male spermatophores, which has been identified by DNA fingerprinting.<sup id="cite_ref-SquiresEtAl2013_85-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SquiresEtAl2013-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> Females are no longer receptive to mating attempts when holding their eggs in their arm. Females can store sperm in two places (1) the buccal cavity where recently mated males place their spermatophores, and (2) the internal sperm-storage receptacles where sperm packages from previous males are stored.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_79-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> Spermatophore storage results in sperm competition; which states that the female controls which mate fertilizes the eggs. In order to reduce this sort of competition, males develop agonistic behaviors like mate guarding and flushing.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_79-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> The <i>Hapalochlaena lunulata</i>, or the blue-ringed octopus, is unable to distinguish between males and females and readily mates with both.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Sexual_dimorphism">Sexual dimorphism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cephalopod&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Sexual dimorphism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In a variety of marine organisms it is seen that females are larger in size compared to the males in some close related species. In some lineages, such as the <a href="/wiki/Blanket_octopus" title="Blanket octopus">blanket octopus</a>, males become structurally smaller and smaller resembling a term, "dwarfism" dwarf males usually occurs at low densities.<sup id="cite_ref-Princeton_University_Press_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Princeton_University_Press-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> The blanket octopus male is an example of sexual-evolutionary dwarfism; females grow 10,000 to 40,000 times larger than the males and the sex ratio between males and females can be distinguished right after hatching of the eggs.<sup id="cite_ref-Princeton_University_Press_90-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Princeton_University_Press-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:SquidEggCases-MontereryAquarium-April2-07.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/SquidEggCases-MontereryAquarium-April2-07.png/220px-SquidEggCases-MontereryAquarium-April2-07.png" width="220" height="161" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/SquidEggCases-MontereryAquarium-April2-07.png/330px-SquidEggCases-MontereryAquarium-April2-07.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/SquidEggCases-MontereryAquarium-April2-07.png/440px-SquidEggCases-MontereryAquarium-April2-07.png 2x" data-file-width="1344" data-file-height="982" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:SquidEggCases-MontereryAquarium-April2-07.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Egg cases laid by a female squid</div></div></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Embryology">Embryology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cephalopod&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Embryology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Cephalopod eggs span a large range of sizes, from 1 to 30&#160;mm in diameter.<sup>[78]</sup> The fertilised <a href="/wiki/Ovum" class="mw-redirect" title="Ovum">ovum</a> initially divides to produce a disc of germinal cells at one pole, with the <a href="/wiki/Yolk" title="Yolk">yolk</a> remaining at the opposite pole. The germinal disc grows to envelop and eventually absorb the yolk, forming the embryo. The tentacles and arms first appear at the hind part of the body, where the foot would be in other molluscs, and only later migrate towards the head.<sup id="cite_ref-IZ_76-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IZ-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Shigeno2008_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shigeno2008-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The funnel of cephalopods develops on the top of their head, whereas the mouth develops on the opposite surface.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>86</span></sup> The early embryological stages are reminiscent of ancestral <a href="/wiki/Gastropod" class="mw-redirect" title="Gastropod">gastropods</a> and extant <a href="/wiki/Monoplacophora" title="Monoplacophora">Monoplacophora</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Shigeno2008_91-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shigeno2008-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The shells develop from the ectoderm as an organic framework which is subsequently mineralised.<sup id="cite_ref-Baratte2007_55-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Baratte2007-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> In <i>Sepia</i>, which has an internal shell, the ectoderm forms an invagination whose pore is sealed off before this organic framework is deposited.<sup id="cite_ref-Baratte2007_55-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Baratte2007-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Development">Development</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cephalopod&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Development">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Chtenopteryx_sicula_paralarvae_-_from_Commons.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/a4/Chtenopteryx_sicula_paralarvae_-_from_Commons.jpg/400px-Chtenopteryx_sicula_paralarvae_-_from_Commons.jpg" width="400" height="159" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/a4/Chtenopteryx_sicula_paralarvae_-_from_Commons.jpg/600px-Chtenopteryx_sicula_paralarvae_-_from_Commons.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/a/a4/Chtenopteryx_sicula_paralarvae_-_from_Commons.jpg 2x" data-file-width="660" data-file-height="262" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Chtenopteryx_sicula_paralarvae_-_from_Commons.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Chtenopteryx_sicula" title="Chtenopteryx sicula">Chtenopteryx sicula</a></i> <a href="/wiki/Paralarva" title="Paralarva">paralarvae</a>. <b>Left:</b> Two very young paralarvae. The circular <a href="/wiki/Tentacular" class="mw-redirect" title="Tentacular">tentacular</a> clubs bear approximately 20 irregularly arranged suckers. Two <a href="/wiki/Chromatophore" title="Chromatophore">chromatophores</a> are present on each side of the <a href="/wiki/Mantle_(mollusc)" title="Mantle (mollusc)">mantle</a>. <b>Centre:</b> Ventral, dorsal and side views of a more advanced paralarva. An equatorial circulet of seven large yellow-brown chromatophores is present on the mantle. Posteriorly the expanded vanes of the gladius are visible in the dorsal view. <b>Right:</b> Ventral and dorsal views of a very advanced paralarva.</div></div></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:334px;max-width:334px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:102px;max-width:102px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Chiroteuthis_veranyi_immature.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Chiroteuthis_veranyi_immature.jpg/100px-Chiroteuthis_veranyi_immature.jpg" width="100" height="311" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Chiroteuthis_veranyi_immature.jpg/150px-Chiroteuthis_veranyi_immature.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Chiroteuthis_veranyi_immature.jpg/200px-Chiroteuthis_veranyi_immature.jpg 2x" data-file-width="466" data-file-height="1448" /></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:228px;max-width:228px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Chiroteuthis_veranyi.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Chiroteuthis_veranyi.jpg/226px-Chiroteuthis_veranyi.jpg" width="226" height="312" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Chiroteuthis_veranyi.jpg/339px-Chiroteuthis_veranyi.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Chiroteuthis_veranyi.jpg/452px-Chiroteuthis_veranyi.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1260" data-file-height="1740" /></a></div></div><div style="clear:left"></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent"><b>Left:</b> Immature specimens of <i><a href="/wiki/Chiroteuthis_veranyi" class="mw-redirect" title="Chiroteuthis veranyi">Chiroteuthis veranyi</a></i>. In this paralarval form, known as the doratopsis stage, the pen is longer than the mantle and 'neck' combined<br />
<b>Right:</b> A mature <i><a href="/wiki/Chiroteuthis_veranyi" class="mw-redirect" title="Chiroteuthis veranyi">Chiroteuthis veranyi</a></i>. This species has some of the longest tentacles in proportion to its size of any known cephalopod.</div></div></div>
<p>The length of time before hatching is highly variable; smaller eggs in warmer waters are the fastest to hatch, and newborns can emerge after as little as a few days.  Larger eggs in colder waters can develop for over a year before hatching.<sup id="cite_ref-Boletzky2009_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Boletzky2009-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The process from spawning to hatching follows a similar trajectory in all species, the main variable being the amount of yolk available to the young and when it is absorbed by the embryo.<sup id="cite_ref-Boletzky2009_93-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Boletzky2009-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Unlike most other molluscs, cephalopods do not have a morphologically distinct <a href="/wiki/Larva" title="Larva">larval</a> stage.  Instead the juveniles are known as <a href="/wiki/Paralarvae" class="mw-redirect" title="Paralarvae">paralarvae</a>.  They quickly learn how to hunt, using encounters with prey to refine their strategies.
</p><p>Growth in juveniles is usually <a href="/wiki/Allometric" class="mw-redirect" title="Allometric">allometric</a>, whilst adult growth is <a href="/wiki/Allometry#Isometric_scaling_and_geometric_similarity" title="Allometry">isometric</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Evolution">Evolution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cephalopod&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Evolution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Evolution_of_cephalopods" title="Evolution of cephalopods">Evolution of cephalopods</a></div>
<p>The traditional view of cephalopod evolution holds that they evolved in the Late Cambrian from a <a href="/wiki/Monoplacophoran" class="mw-redirect" title="Monoplacophoran">monoplacophoran</a>-like ancestor<sup id="cite_ref-Lemche1959_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lemche1959-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> with a curved, tapering shell,<sup id="cite_ref-Wingstrand1985_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wingstrand1985-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> which was closely related to the <a href="/wiki/Gastropod" class="mw-redirect" title="Gastropod">gastropods</a> (snails).<sup id="cite_ref-Boyle3_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Boyle3-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> The similarity of the early shelled cephalopod <i><a href="/wiki/Plectronoceras" title="Plectronoceras">Plectronoceras</a></i> to some gastropods was used in support of this view. The development of a <a href="/wiki/Siphuncle" title="Siphuncle">siphuncle</a> would have allowed the shells of these early forms to become gas-filled (thus buoyant) in order to support them and keep the shells upright while the animal crawled along the floor, and separated the true cephalopods from putative ancestors such as <i><a href="/wiki/Knightoconus" title="Knightoconus">Knightoconus</a></i>, which lacked a siphuncle.<sup id="cite_ref-Boyle3_97-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Boyle3-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> Neutral or positive buoyancy (i.e. the ability to float) would have come later, followed by swimming in the <a href="/wiki/Plectronocerida" title="Plectronocerida">Plectronocerida</a> and eventually jet propulsion in more derived cephalopods.<sup id="cite_ref-Kroger2007_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kroger2007-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>However, some morphological evidence is difficult to reconcile with this view, and the redescription of <i><a href="/wiki/Nectocaris" title="Nectocaris">Nectocaris pteryx</a></i>, which did not have a shell and appeared to possess jet propulsion in the manner of "derived" cephalopods, complicated the question of the order in which cephalopod features developed – provided <i>Nectocaris</i> is a cephalopod at all.<sup id="cite_ref-SmithAndCaron2010_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SmithAndCaron2010-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> Their position within the Mollusca is currently wide open to interpretation – see <a href="/wiki/Mollusca#Phylogeny" title="Mollusca">Mollusca#Phylogeny</a>.
</p><p>Early cephalopods were likely predators near the top of the food chain.<sup id="cite_ref-Boyle_18-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Boyle-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> After the <a href="/wiki/Cambrian%E2%80%93Ordovician_extinction_event" title="Cambrian–Ordovician extinction event">late Cambrian extinction</a> led to the disappearance of many <a href="/wiki/Anomalocaridid" title="Anomalocaridid">Anomalocaridids</a>, predatory niches became available for other animals.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> During the Ordovician period the primitive cephalopods underwent pulses of diversification<sup id="cite_ref-Kroger2009_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kroger2009-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> to become diverse and dominant in the <a href="/wiki/Paleozoic" title="Paleozoic">Paleozoic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mesozoic" title="Mesozoic">Mesozoic</a> seas.<sup id="cite_ref-Dzik1981_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dzik1981-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the Early Palaeozoic, their range was far more restricted than today; they were mainly constrained to sublittoral regions of shallow shelves of the low latitudes, and usually occurred in association with <a href="/wiki/Thrombolites" class="mw-redirect" title="Thrombolites">thrombolites</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Kroger2009b_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kroger2009b-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> A more pelagic habit was gradually adopted as the Ordovician progressed.<sup id="cite_ref-Kroger2009b_103-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kroger2009b-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> Deep-water cephalopods, whilst rare, have been found in the Lower Ordovician – but only in high-latitude waters.<sup id="cite_ref-Kroger2009b_103-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kroger2009b-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup>
The mid Ordovician saw the first cephalopods with septa strong enough to cope with the pressures associated with deeper water, and could inhabit depths greater than 100–200&#160;m.<sup id="cite_ref-Kroger2009_101-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kroger2009-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> The direction of shell coiling would prove to be crucial to the future success of the lineages; endogastric coiling would only permit large size to be attained with a straight shell, whereas exogastric coiling – initially rather rare – permitted the spirals familiar from the fossil record to develop, with their corresponding large size and diversity.<sup id="cite_ref-Holland1986_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Holland1986-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> (Endogastric mean the shell is curved so as the ventral or lower side is longitudinally concave (belly in); exogastric means the shell is curved so as the ventral side is longitudinally convex (belly out) allowing the funnel to be pointed backwards beneath the shell.)<sup id="cite_ref-Holland1986_104-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Holland1986-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a href="/wiki/File:1212amma.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/1212amma.jpg/260px-1212amma.jpg" width="260" height="97" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/1212amma.jpg/390px-1212amma.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/1212amma.jpg/520px-1212amma.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="238" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:1212amma.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An <a href="/wiki/Ammonoid" class="mw-redirect" title="Ammonoid">ammonoid</a> with the body chamber missing, showing the septal surface (especially at right) with its undulating lobes and saddles</div></div></div>
<p>The ancestors of coleoids (including most modern cephalopods) and the ancestors of the modern nautilus, had diverged by the Floian Age of the Early Ordovician Period, over 470 million years ago.<sup id="cite_ref-Kroger2009b_103-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kroger2009b-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kröger2006_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kröger2006-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Bactritida" title="Bactritida">Bactritida</a>, a Silurian–Triassic group of orthocones, are widely held to be paraphyletic to the coleoids and ammonoids, that is, the latter groups arose from within the Bactritida.<sup id="cite_ref-Young1998_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Young1998-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>393</span></sup>  An increase in the diversity of the coleoids and ammonoids is observed around the start of the Devonian period, and corresponds with a profound increase in fish diversity.  This could represent the origin of the two derived groups.<sup id="cite_ref-Young1998_106-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Young1998-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Unlike most modern cephalopods, most ancient varieties had protective shells. These shells at first were conical but later developed into curved nautiloid shapes seen in modern <a href="/wiki/Nautilus" title="Nautilus">nautilus</a> species.
Competitive pressure from fish is thought to have forced the shelled forms into deeper water, which provided an evolutionary pressure towards shell loss and gave rise to the modern coleoids, a change which led to greater metabolic costs associated with the loss of buoyancy, but which allowed them to recolonise shallow waters.<sup id="cite_ref-Boyle3_97-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Boyle3-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>36</span></sup> However, some of the straight-shelled <a href="/wiki/Nautiloid" title="Nautiloid">nautiloids</a> evolved into <a href="/wiki/Belemnitida" title="Belemnitida">belemnites</a>, out of which some evolved into <a href="/wiki/Squid" title="Squid">squid</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cuttlefish" title="Cuttlefish">cuttlefish</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag needs to be fact-checked with the cited source(s). (November 2008)">verification needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>  The loss of the shell may also have resulted from evolutionary pressure to increase manoeuvrability, resulting in a more fish-like habit.<sup id="cite_ref-mollusca11_1-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mollusca11-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>289</span></sup>
</p><p>There has been debate on the <a href="/wiki/Embryogenesis" title="Embryogenesis">embryological origin</a> of cephalopod appendages.<sup id="cite_ref-Tanabe_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tanabe-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> Until the mid-twentieth century, the "Arms as Head" hypothesis was widely recognized. In this theory, the arms and tentacles of cephalopods look similar to the head appendages of gastropods, suggesting that they might be <a href="/wiki/Homology_(biology)" title="Homology (biology)">homologous structures</a>. Cephalopod appendages surround the mouth, so logically they could be derived from embryonic head tissues.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> However, the "Arms as Foot" hypothesis, proposed by <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Naef" title="Adolf Naef">Adolf Naef</a> in 1928, has increasingly been favoured;<sup id="cite_ref-Tanabe_107-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tanabe-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> for example, <a href="/wiki/Fate_mapping" title="Fate mapping">fate mapping</a> of limb buds in the <a href="/wiki/Chambered_nautilus" title="Chambered nautilus">chambered nautilus</a> indicates that limb buds originate from "foot" embryonic tissues.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Phylogeny">Phylogeny</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cephalopod&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Phylogeny">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The approximate consensus of extant cephalopod phylogeny, after Strugnell <i>et al</i>. 2007, is shown in the <a href="/wiki/Cladogram" title="Cladogram">cladogram</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Strugnell2007_61-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Strugnell2007-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> Mineralized taxa are in <b>bold</b>.  The attachment of the clade including <i>Sepia</i> and <i>Spirula</i> is unclear; either of the points marked with an asterisk may represent the root of this clade.

<p>The internal phylogeny of the cephalopods is difficult to constrain; many molecular techniques have been adopted, but the results produced are conflicting.<sup id="cite_ref-Strugnell2007_61-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Strugnell2007-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Strugnell2005_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Strugnell2005-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup>  <i>Nautilus</i> tends to be considered an outgroup, with <i><a href="/wiki/Vampyroteuthis" class="mw-redirect" title="Vampyroteuthis">Vampyroteuthis</a></i> forming an outgroup to other squid; however in one analysis the nautiloids, octopus and teuthids plot as a polytomy.<sup id="cite_ref-Strugnell2007_61-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Strugnell2007-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> Some molecular phylogenies do not recover the mineralized coleoids (<i>Spirula</i>, <i>Sepia</i>, and <i>Metasepia</i>) as a clade; however, others do recover this more parsimonious-seeming clade, with <i>Spirula</i> as a sister group to <i>Sepia</i> and <i>Metasepia</i> in a clade that had probably diverged before the end of the Triassic.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Molecular estimates for clade divergence vary.  One 'statistically robust' estimate has <i>Nautilus</i> diverging from <i>Octopus</i> at <span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=415">415</a>&#160;&#177;&#160;24&#160;million years ago</span>.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Taxonomy">Taxonomy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cephalopod&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Taxonomy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:232px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sepia_officinalis_(aquarium).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Sepia_officinalis_%28aquarium%29.jpg/230px-Sepia_officinalis_%28aquarium%29.jpg" width="230" height="173" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Sepia_officinalis_%28aquarium%29.jpg/345px-Sepia_officinalis_%28aquarium%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Sepia_officinalis_%28aquarium%29.jpg/460px-Sepia_officinalis_%28aquarium%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3408" data-file-height="2556" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sepia_officinalis_(aquarium).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Common_cuttlefish" title="Common cuttlefish">Common cuttlefish</a> (<i>Sepia officinalis</i>)</div></div></div>

<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:232px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Octopus_vulgaris2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Octopus_vulgaris2.jpg/230px-Octopus_vulgaris2.jpg" width="230" height="153" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Octopus_vulgaris2.jpg/345px-Octopus_vulgaris2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Octopus_vulgaris2.jpg/460px-Octopus_vulgaris2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1465" data-file-height="977" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Octopus_vulgaris2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Common_octopus" title="Common octopus">Common octopus</a> (<i>Octopus vulgaris</i>)</div></div></div>
<p>The classification presented here, for recent cephalopods, follows largely from <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.mnh.si.edu/cephs/newclass.pdf">Current Classification of Recent Cephalopoda</a> (May 2001), for fossil cephalopods takes from Arkell et al. 1957, Teichert and Moore 1964, Teichert 1988, and others.  The three subclasses are traditional, corresponding to the three orders of cephalopods recognized by Bather.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><b>Class Cephalopoda</b> († indicates <a href="/wiki/Extinct" class="mw-redirect" title="Extinct">extinct</a> groups)
</p>
<ul><li>Subclass <a href="/wiki/Nautiloid" title="Nautiloid">Nautiloidea</a>: Fundamental  ectocochliate cephalopods that provided the source for the Ammonoidea and Coleoidea.
<ul><li>Order † <a href="/wiki/Plectronocerida" title="Plectronocerida">Plectronocerida</a>: the ancestral cephalopods from the <a href="/wiki/Cambrian" title="Cambrian">Cambrian</a> Period</li>
<li>Order † <a href="/wiki/Ellesmerocerida" title="Ellesmerocerida">Ellesmerocerida</a> (<span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=500–470">500&#160;to&#160;470</a> Ma</span>)</li>
<li>Order † <a href="/wiki/Endocerida" title="Endocerida">Endocerida</a> (<span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=485–430">485&#160;to&#160;430</a> Ma</span>)</li>
<li>Order † <a href="/wiki/Actinocerida" title="Actinocerida">Actinocerida</a> (<span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=480–312">480&#160;to&#160;312</a> Ma</span>)</li>
<li>Order † <a href="/wiki/Discosorida" title="Discosorida">Discosorida</a> (<span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=482–392">482&#160;to&#160;392</a> Ma</span>)</li>
<li>Order † <a href="/wiki/Pseudorthocerida" title="Pseudorthocerida">Pseudorthocerida</a> (<span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=432–272">432&#160;to&#160;272</a> Ma</span>)</li>
<li>Order † <a href="/wiki/Tarphycerida" title="Tarphycerida">Tarphycerida</a> (<span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=485–386">485&#160;to&#160;386</a> Ma</span>)</li>
<li>Order † <a href="/wiki/Oncocerida" title="Oncocerida">Oncocerida</a> (<span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=478.5–324.0">478.5&#160;to&#160;324</a> Ma</span>)</li>
<li>Order <a href="/wiki/Nautilida" title="Nautilida">Nautilida</a> (extant; 410.5 Ma to present)</li>
<li>Order † <a href="/wiki/Orthocerida" title="Orthocerida">Orthocerida</a> (<span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=482.5–211.5">482.5&#160;to&#160;211.5</a> Ma</span>)</li>
<li>Order † <a href="/wiki/Ascocerida" title="Ascocerida">Ascocerida</a> (<span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=478–412">478&#160;to&#160;412</a> Ma</span>)</li>
<li>Order † <a href="/wiki/Bactritida" title="Bactritida">Bactritida</a> (<span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=418.1–260.5">418.1&#160;to&#160;260.5</a> Ma</span>)</li></ul></li>
<li>Subclass † <a href="/wiki/Ammonoidea" title="Ammonoidea">Ammonoidea</a>: Ammonites (<span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=479–66">479&#160;to&#160;66</a> Ma</span>)
<ul><li>Order † <a href="/wiki/Goniatitida" class="mw-redirect" title="Goniatitida">Goniatitida</a> (<span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=388.5–252.0">388.5&#160;to&#160;252</a> Ma</span>)</li>
<li>Order † <a href="/wiki/Ceratitida" title="Ceratitida">Ceratitida</a> (<span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=254–200">254&#160;to&#160;200</a> Ma</span>)</li>
<li>Order † <a href="/wiki/Ammonitida" title="Ammonitida">Ammonitida</a> (<span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=215–66">215&#160;to&#160;66</a> Ma</span>)</li></ul></li>
<li>Subclass <a href="/wiki/Coleoidea" title="Coleoidea">Coleoidea</a> (410.0 Ma-Rec)
<ul><li>Cohort † <a href="/wiki/Belemnoidea" title="Belemnoidea">Belemnoidea</a>: Belemnites and kin
<ul><li>Genus † <i><a href="/wiki/Jeletzkya" title="Jeletzkya">Jeletzkya</a></i></li>
<li>Order † <a href="/wiki/Aulacocerida" title="Aulacocerida">Aulacocerida</a> (<span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=265–183">265&#160;to&#160;183</a> Ma</span>)</li>
<li>Order † <a href="/wiki/Phragmoteuthida" title="Phragmoteuthida">Phragmoteuthida</a> (<span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=189.6–183.0">189.6&#160;to&#160;183</a> Ma</span>)</li>
<li>Order † <a href="/wiki/Hematitida" title="Hematitida">Hematitida</a> (<span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=339.4–318.1">339.4&#160;to&#160;318.1</a> Ma</span>)</li>
<li>Order † <a href="/wiki/Belemnitida" title="Belemnitida">Belemnitida</a> (<span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=339.4–66">339.4&#160;to&#160;66</a> Ma</span>)</li>
<li>Genus † <i><a href="/wiki/Belemnoteuthis" class="mw-redirect" title="Belemnoteuthis">Belemnoteuthis</a></i> (<span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=189.6–183.0">189.6&#160;to&#160;183</a> Ma</span>)</li></ul></li>
<li>Cohort <a href="/wiki/Neocoleoidea" title="Neocoleoidea">Neocoleoidea</a>
<ul><li>Superorder <a href="/wiki/Decapodiformes" title="Decapodiformes">Decapodiformes</a> (also known as Decabrachia or Decembranchiata)
<ul><li>?Order † <a href="/wiki/Boletzkyida" title="Boletzkyida">Boletzkyida</a></li>
<li>Order <a href="/wiki/Spirulida" title="Spirulida">Spirulida</a>: Ram's horn squid</li>
<li>Order <a href="/wiki/Sepiida" class="mw-redirect" title="Sepiida">Sepiida</a>: cuttlefish</li>
<li>Order <a href="/wiki/Sepiolida" class="mw-redirect" title="Sepiolida">Sepiolida</a>: pygmy, bobtail and bottletail squid</li>
<li>Order <a href="/wiki/Teuthida" class="mw-redirect" title="Teuthida">Teuthida</a>: squid</li></ul></li>
<li>Superorder <a href="/wiki/Octopodiformes" title="Octopodiformes">Octopodiformes</a> (also known as Vampyropoda)
<ul><li>Family † <a href="/wiki/Trachyteuthididae" class="mw-redirect" title="Trachyteuthididae">Trachyteuthididae</a></li>
<li>Order <a href="/wiki/Vampyromorphida" title="Vampyromorphida">Vampyromorphida</a>: Vampire squid</li>
<li>Order <a href="/wiki/Octopoda" class="mw-redirect" title="Octopoda">Octopoda</a>: octopus</li></ul></li></ul></li></ul></li></ul>
<p>Other classifications differ, primarily in how the various <a href="/wiki/Decapodiformes" title="Decapodiformes">decapod</a> orders are related, and whether they should be orders or families.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Suprafamilial_classification_of_the_Treatise">Suprafamilial classification of the Treatise</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cephalopod&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Suprafamilial classification of the Treatise">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>This is the older classification that combines those found in parts K and L of the <i>Treatise on Invertebrate Paleontology</i>, which forms the basis for and is retained in large part by classifications that have come later.
</p><p>Nautiloids in general (Teichert and Moore, 1964) sequence as given.
</p>
<dl><dd>Subclass † <a href="/wiki/Endocerida" title="Endocerida">Endoceratoidea</a>.    Not used by Flower, e.g. Flower and Kummel 1950, interjocerids included in the Endocerida.
<dl><dd>Order † <a href="/wiki/Endocerida" title="Endocerida">Endocerida</a></dd>
<dd>Order † <a href="/wiki/Intejocerida" title="Intejocerida">Intejocerida</a></dd></dl></dd></dl>
<dl><dd>Subclass † <a href="/wiki/Actinocerida" title="Actinocerida">Actinoceratoidea</a> Not used by Flower, ibid
<dl><dd>Order † <a href="/wiki/Actinocerida" title="Actinocerida">Actinocerida</a></dd></dl></dd></dl>
<dl><dd>Subclass † <a href="/wiki/Nautiloid" title="Nautiloid">Nautiloidea</a> Nautiloidea in the restricted sense.
<dl><dd>Order † <a href="/wiki/Ellesmerocerida" title="Ellesmerocerida">Ellesmerocerida</a> Plectronocerida subsequently split off as separate order.</dd>
<dd>Order † <a href="/wiki/Orthocerida" title="Orthocerida">Orthocerida</a> Includes orthocerids and pseudorthocerids</dd>
<dd>Order † <a href="/wiki/Ascocerida" title="Ascocerida">Ascocerida</a></dd>
<dd>Order † <a href="/wiki/Oncocerida" title="Oncocerida">Oncocerida</a></dd>
<dd>Order † <a href="/wiki/Discosorida" title="Discosorida">Discosorida</a></dd>
<dd>Order † <a href="/wiki/Tarphycerida" title="Tarphycerida">Tarphycerida</a></dd>
<dd>Order † <a href="/wiki/Tarphycerida" title="Tarphycerida">Barrandeocerida</a> A polyphyletic group now included in the Tarphycerida</dd>
<dd>Order <a href="/wiki/Nautilida" title="Nautilida">Nautilida</a></dd></dl></dd></dl>
<dl><dd>Subclass † <a href="/wiki/Bactritida" title="Bactritida">Bactritoidea</a>
<dl><dd>Order † <a href="/wiki/Bactritida" title="Bactritida">Bactritida</a></dd></dl></dd></dl>
<p>Paleozoic Ammonoidea (Miller, Furnish and Schindewolf, 1957)
</p>
<dl><dd><dl><dd>Suborder † <a href="/wiki/Anarcestida" class="mw-redirect" title="Anarcestida">Anarcestina</a></dd>
<dd>Suborder † <a href="/wiki/Clymeniida" title="Clymeniida">Clymeniina</a></dd>
<dd>Suborder † <a href="/wiki/Goniatitida" class="mw-redirect" title="Goniatitida">Goniatitina</a></dd>
<dd>Suborder † <a href="/wiki/Prolecanitida" title="Prolecanitida">Prolecanitina</a></dd></dl></dd></dl>
<p>Mesozoic Ammonoidea (Arkel et al., 1957)
</p>
<dl><dd><dl><dd>Suborder † <a href="/wiki/Ceratitida" title="Ceratitida">Ceratitina</a></dd>
<dd>Suborder † <a href="/wiki/Phylloceratida" class="mw-redirect" title="Phylloceratida">Phylloceratina</a></dd>
<dd>Suborder † <a href="/wiki/Lytoceratida" class="mw-redirect" title="Lytoceratida">Lytoceratina</a></dd>
<dd>Suborder † <a href="/wiki/Ammonitida" title="Ammonitida">Ammonitina</a></dd></dl></dd></dl>
<p>Subsequent revisions include the establishment of three Upper Cambrian orders, the Plectronocerida, Protactinocerida and Yanhecerida; separation of the pseudorthocerids as the Pseudorthocerida, and elevating orthoceritoids as the Subclass Orthoceratoidea.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Shevyrev_classification">Shevyrev classification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cephalopod&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Shevyrev classification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Shevyrev (2005) suggested a division into eight subclasses, mostly comprising the more diverse and numerous fossil forms,<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> although this classification has been criticized as arbitrary.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<p><b>Class Cephalopoda</b>
</p>
<ul><li>Subclass † <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ellesmeroceratoidea&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Ellesmeroceratoidea (page does not exist)">Ellesmeroceratoidea</a>
<ul><li>Order † <a href="/wiki/Plectronocerida" title="Plectronocerida">Plectronocerida</a> (<span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=501–490">501&#160;to&#160;490</a> Ma</span>)</li>
<li>Order † <a href="/w/index.php?title=Protactinocerida&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Protactinocerida (page does not exist)">Protactinocerida</a></li>

<li>Order † <a href="/wiki/Ellesmerocerida" title="Ellesmerocerida">Ellesmerocerida</a> (<span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=500–470">500&#160;to&#160;470</a> Ma</span>)</li></ul></li>
<li>Subclass † <a href="/wiki/Endocerida" title="Endocerida">Endoceratoidea</a> (<span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=485–430">485&#160;to&#160;430</a> Ma</span>)
<ul><li>Order † <a href="/wiki/Endocerida" title="Endocerida">Endocerida</a> (<span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=485–430">485&#160;to&#160;430</a> Ma</span>)</li>
<li>Order † <a href="/wiki/Intejocerida" title="Intejocerida">Intejocerida</a> (<span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=485–480">485&#160;to&#160;480</a> Ma</span>)</li></ul></li>
<li>Subclass † <a href="/wiki/Actinocerida" title="Actinocerida">Actinoceratoidea</a>
<ul><li>Order † <a href="/wiki/Actinocerida" title="Actinocerida">Actinocerida</a> (<span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=480–312">480&#160;to&#160;312</a> Ma</span>)</li></ul></li>
<li>Subclass <a href="/wiki/Nautiloid" title="Nautiloid">Nautiloidea</a> (490.0 Ma- Rec)
<ul><li>Order † <a href="/wiki/Basslerocerida" title="Basslerocerida">Basslerocerida</a> (<span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=490–480">490&#160;to&#160;480</a> Ma</span>)</li>
<li>Order † <a href="/wiki/Tarphycerida" title="Tarphycerida">Tarphycerida</a> (<span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=485–386">485&#160;to&#160;386</a> Ma</span>)</li>
<li>Order † <a href="/wiki/Lituitida" title="Lituitida">Lituitida</a> (<span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=485–480">485&#160;to&#160;480</a> Ma</span>)</li>
<li>Order † <a href="/wiki/Discosorida" title="Discosorida">Discosorida</a> (<span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=482–392">482&#160;to&#160;392</a> Ma</span>)</li>
<li>Order † <a href="/wiki/Oncocerida" title="Oncocerida">Oncocerida</a> (<span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=478.5–324.0">478.5&#160;to&#160;324</a> Ma</span>)</li>
<li>Order <a href="/wiki/Nautilida" title="Nautilida">Nautilida</a> (410.5 Ma-Rec)</li></ul></li>
<li>Subclass † <a href="/wiki/Orthoceratoidea" title="Orthoceratoidea">Orthoceratoidea</a> (<span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=482.5–211.5">482.5&#160;to&#160;211.5</a> Ma</span>)
<ul><li>Order † <a href="/wiki/Orthocerida" title="Orthocerida">Orthocerida</a> (<span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=482.5–211.5">482.5&#160;to&#160;211.5</a> Ma</span>)</li>
<li>Order † <a href="/wiki/Ascocerida" title="Ascocerida">Ascocerida</a> (<span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=478–412">478&#160;to&#160;412</a> Ma</span>)</li>
<li>Order † <a href="/wiki/Dissidocerida" title="Dissidocerida">Dissidocerida</a> (<span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=479.0–457.5">479&#160;to&#160;457.5</a> Ma</span>)</li>
<li>Order † <a href="/wiki/Bajkaloceras" title="Bajkaloceras">Bajkalocerida</a></li></ul></li>
<li>Subclass † <a href="/wiki/Bactritoidea" class="mw-redirect" title="Bactritoidea">Bactritoidea</a> (<span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=422–252">422&#160;to&#160;252</a> Ma</span>)</li>
<li>Subclass † <a href="/wiki/Ammonoidea" title="Ammonoidea">Ammonoidea</a> (<span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=410–66">410&#160;to&#160;66</a> Ma</span>)</li>
<li>Subclass <a href="/wiki/Coleoidea" title="Coleoidea">Coleoidea</a> (410.0 Ma-rec)<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cladistic_classification">Cladistic classification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cephalopod&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Cladistic classification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Vampylarge.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Vampylarge.JPG/250px-Vampylarge.JPG" width="250" height="143" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Vampylarge.JPG/375px-Vampylarge.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Vampylarge.JPG/500px-Vampylarge.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1503" data-file-height="860" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Vampylarge.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Pyritized fossil of <i><a href="/wiki/Vampyronassa_rhodanica" class="mw-redirect" title="Vampyronassa rhodanica">Vampyronassa rhodanica</a></i>, a <a href="/wiki/Vampyromorphida" title="Vampyromorphida">vampyromorphid</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Callovian" title="Callovian">Lower Callovian</a> (<span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=166.1">166.1</a>&#160;million years ago</span>)</div></div></div>
<p>Another recent system divides all cephalopods into two <a href="/wiki/Clade" title="Clade">clades</a>.  One includes nautilus and most fossil nautiloids.  The other clade (<a href="/wiki/Neocephalopoda" title="Neocephalopoda">Neocephalopoda</a> or Angusteradulata) is closer to modern coleoids, and includes belemnoids, ammonoids, and many <a href="/wiki/Orthocerid" class="mw-redirect" title="Orthocerid">orthocerid</a> families.  There are also <a href="/wiki/Stem_group" class="mw-redirect" title="Stem group">stem group</a> cephalopods of the traditional <a href="/wiki/Ellesmerocerida" title="Ellesmerocerida">Ellesmerocerida</a> that belong to neither clade.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The coleoids, despite some doubts,<sup id="cite_ref-mollusca11_1-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mollusca11-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>289</span></sup> appear from molecular data to be monophyletic.<sup id="cite_ref-Lindgren2004_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lindgren2004-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_culture">In culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cephalopod&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: In culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Cephalopods_in_popular_culture" title="Cephalopods in popular culture">Cephalopods in popular culture</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Colossal_octopus_by_Pierre_Denys_de_Montfort.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Coloured drawing of a huge octopus rising from the sea and attacking a sailing ship&#39;s three masts with its spiralling arms" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Colossal_octopus_by_Pierre_Denys_de_Montfort.jpg/170px-Colossal_octopus_by_Pierre_Denys_de_Montfort.jpg" width="170" height="261" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/9d/Colossal_octopus_by_Pierre_Denys_de_Montfort.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="225" data-file-height="346" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Colossal_octopus_by_Pierre_Denys_de_Montfort.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Pen and <a href="/wiki/Wash_(visual_arts)" title="Wash (visual arts)">wash</a> drawing of an imagined colossal octopus attacking a ship, by the <a href="/wiki/Malacologist" class="mw-redirect" title="Malacologist">malacologist</a> <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Denys_de_Montfort" title="Pierre Denys de Montfort">Pierre de Montfort</a>, 1801</div></div></div>
<p>Ancient seafaring people were aware of cephalopods, as evidenced by artworks such as a stone carving found in the archaeological recovery from Bronze Age <a href="/wiki/Minoan_civilization" title="Minoan civilization">Minoan Crete</a> at <a href="/wiki/Knossos" title="Knossos">Knossos</a> (1900 – 1100 BC) has a depiction of a fisherman carrying an octopus.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> The terrifyingly powerful <a href="/wiki/Gorgon" title="Gorgon">Gorgon</a> of <a href="/wiki/Greek_mythology" title="Greek mythology">Greek mythology</a> may have been inspired by the octopus or squid, the octopus's body representing the severed head of <a href="/wiki/Medusa" title="Medusa">Medusa</a>, the beak as the protruding tongue and fangs, and its tentacles as the snakes.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:NROL-39_Patch.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A mission badge of an octopus spanning the world against a starry background, labelled &quot;NROL-39&quot; and &quot;Nothing is beyond our reach&quot;" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/NROL-39_Patch.jpg/220px-NROL-39_Patch.jpg" width="220" height="214" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/NROL-39_Patch.jpg/330px-NROL-39_Patch.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/cb/NROL-39_Patch.jpg 2x" data-file-width="349" data-file-height="340" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:NROL-39_Patch.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/NROL-39" class="mw-redirect" title="NROL-39">NROL-39</a> mission patch, depicting the <a href="/wiki/National_Reconnaissance_Office" title="National Reconnaissance Office">National Reconnaissance Office</a> as an octopus with a long reach</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Kraken" title="Kraken">Kraken</a> are legendary sea monsters of giant proportions said to dwell off the coasts of Norway and Greenland, usually portrayed in art as giant cephalopods attacking ships. <a href="/wiki/Linnaeus" class="mw-redirect" title="Linnaeus">Linnaeus</a> included it in the first edition of his 1735 <i><a href="/wiki/Systema_Naturae" title="Systema Naturae">Systema Naturae</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-metropolitana_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-metropolitana-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> A Hawaiian <a href="/wiki/Creation_myth" title="Creation myth">creation myth</a> says that the present cosmos is the last of a series which arose in stages from the ruins of the previous universe. In this account, the octopus is the lone survivor of the previous, alien universe.<sup id="cite_ref-Dixon_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dixon-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Akkorokamui" title="Akkorokamui">Akkorokamui</a> is a gigantic tentacled <a href="/wiki/Monster" title="Monster">monster</a> from <a href="/wiki/Ainu_people" title="Ainu people">Ainu</a> folklore.<sup id="cite_ref-Batchelor_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Batchelor-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A battle with an octopus plays a significant role in <a href="/wiki/Victor_Hugo" title="Victor Hugo">Victor Hugo</a>'s book <i>Travailleurs de la mer</i> (<i><a href="/wiki/Toilers_of_the_Sea" title="Toilers of the Sea">Toilers of the Sea</a></i>), relating to his time in exile on <a href="/wiki/Guernsey" title="Guernsey">Guernsey</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> 
<a href="/wiki/Ian_Fleming" title="Ian Fleming">Ian Fleming</a>'s 1966 short story collection <i><a href="/wiki/Octopussy_and_The_Living_Daylights" title="Octopussy and The Living Daylights">Octopussy and The Living Daylights</a></i>, and the 1983 <a href="/wiki/Octopussy" title="Octopussy"><i>James Bond</i> film</a> were partly inspired by Hugo's book.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Japanese erotic art, <i><a href="/wiki/Shunga" title="Shunga">shunga</a></i>, includes <a href="/wiki/Ukiyo-e" title="Ukiyo-e">ukiyo-e</a> woodblock prints such as <a href="/wiki/Katsushika_Hokusai" class="mw-redirect" title="Katsushika Hokusai">Katsushika Hokusai</a>'s 1814 print <i>Tako to ama</i> (<a href="/wiki/The_Dream_of_the_Fisherman%27s_Wife" title="The Dream of the Fisherman&#39;s Wife">The Dream of the Fisherman's Wife</a>), in which an <a href="/wiki/Ama_diver" class="mw-redirect" title="Ama diver">ama diver</a> is sexually intertwined with a large and a small octopus.<sup id="cite_ref-Helsinki_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Helsinki-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> The print is a forerunner of <a href="/wiki/Tentacle_erotica" title="Tentacle erotica">tentacle erotica</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Briel_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Briel-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> The biologist <a href="/wiki/P._Z._Myers" class="mw-redirect" title="P. Z. Myers">P. Z. Myers</a> noted in his science blog, <a href="/wiki/Pharyngula_(blog)" title="Pharyngula (blog)">Pharyngula</a>, that octopuses appear in "extraordinary" graphic illustrations involving women, tentacles, and bare breasts.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Since it has numerous arms emanating from a common centre, the octopus is often used as a symbol for a powerful and manipulative organisation, usually negatively.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
